<span id="freeTextContainer5410511580624941209"><b>Based on the true struggles of the country of Colombia to rid itself of the drug.<br/>Child soldiers recruited to fight other teenagers in one big cycle of interconnected violence in a struggle for power, financed by cocaine trafficking. The innocent, and not so innocent people of Columbia were caught in the middle of a war between the drug lords, traffickers, police, army and the paramilitary groups.<br/>And yet a love story blooms on this battlefield . . .</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576915860i/28645476.jpg' width='450' height='375' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><i>A typical teenager dreams of having a lovely gir</i></span>
  <span id="freeText5410511580624941209" style="display:none"><b>Based on the true struggles of the country of Colombia to rid itself of the drug.<br>Child soldiers recruited to fight other teenagers in one big cycle of interconnected violence in a struggle for power, financed by cocaine trafficking. The innocent, and not so innocent people of Columbia were caught in the middle of a war between the drug lords, traffickers, police, army and the paramilitary groups.<br>And yet a love story blooms on this battlefield . . .</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576915860i/28645476.jpg" width="450" height="375" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>A typical teenager dreams of having a lovely girlfriend</i><br>It seemed everyone wanted to leave Llorona, and they wanted me to leave too. Palillo wanted me to join the Paramilitaries. As for Camila, she wanted to study fine arts in BogotÃ¡. Weâd been dating for two years but already sheâd mapped out our lives. Top of her grade, she was going to university. If her family couldnât afford the tuition fees, weâd run away and live in a studio apartment in the capital, catch a bus to work in the morning, cook dinner over a tiny stove and scrimp and save for utility bills.<br><br><i>The war comes too close</i><br>They came on a Wednesday to execute my father.<br>Looking back, I should have sensed something amiss during morning Mass three days earlier. The new priestâs maiden sermon had left the congregation divided â some bored, some irate â never a good omen in a small Colombian town.<br>When the congregation rose to leave, SeÃ±or MuÃ±oz, the father of my girlfriend Camila, paused briefly in the aisle and leaned towards PapÃ¡.<br>âMay I talk to you outside?â Glancing at me, he added, âIn private.â<br>I was fifteen years old and in adolescent limbo: not old enough to be included in adult discussions yet not young enough to run off and play. While the grown-ups talked, I stood shiftily on the church steps with Camila and my best friend, Palillo, waiting for them to finish.<br><br> . . . <br><br>PapÃ¡âs conversation with SeÃ±or MuÃ±oz on the church steps that day was merely the latest in a series of warning signs that had begun gathering like slow-circling vultures over an injured animal. First, the 3.30 am cylinder bombs that had rained down on Lloronaâs main street a month earlier. Second, the clandestine night-time burial of Farmer DÃ­az, whoâd been kidnapped and then murdered by the Guerrilla. And third, the bullet through the churchâs stained-glass window that had prompted the old priestâs transfer to BogotÃ¡ for reasons of personal safety.<br>It was all big news. It was all connected. And it was all leading up to something bigger.<br><br><i>A history lesson</i><br>PapÃ¡ explained that war was not a game. For over a decade, he said, the Guerrilla had controlled the three river villages to the south of Llorona. The army controlled Garbanzos, the nearest major town. But Llorona was different. The army patrolled inside the townâs perimeter; the Guerrilla controlled the surrounding countryside. Over the years, the warring sides had reached an informal truce: the Guerrilla didnât attack Llorona and the army didnât go looking for them or their camps. Our finca, or farm, was four kilometres from the plaza. As such, we lived in the grey area on the border between two enemies and had to contend with pressure from both groups.<br>After PapÃ¡âs explanation, I began seeing things properly. Iâd always thought the soldiers who crossed our land were from the armyâs battalion in Garbanzos. Some of them were, but others were members of the armyâs enemy, the communist Guerrilla. The army and Guerrilla looked similar. Both had short haircuts, wore green camouflage uniforms and hats, and carried rifles. PapÃ¡, whoâd always told me to go inside whenever they came, now kept me next to him.<br><br><i>Choose a side</i><br>Palillo hated the Guerrilla for causing his fatherâs death. And since his fatherâs death had caused his stepfatherâs arrival, he hated them even more. Not to mention how much he hated them for causing his move to Llorona. He said it was a shitty town with shitty people, where there was nothing to do except smoke cigarettes. He wanted out. But boys in Llorona knew there were only two ways to leave town â the Guerrilla or the army. Eighteen was the minimum age to join the army; Palillo had already presented himself twice at the gates of the army barracks, claiming to have lost his birth certificate, to no avail. Now, with the arrival of the Paramilitaries in Garbanzos, there was a third option.<br>The Paramilitaries had no minimum age; you simply had to be big enough to carry a pack and fire a rifle. Those who left rarely returned to Llorona â it was too dangerous if the Guerrilla found out â although some had been sighted in Garbanzos. They now cruised around in four-wheel drives or rode motorbikes. They wore new sneakers and jeans with the latest cell phones hooked to their belts. Under their shirts, many concealed pistols. They drank beer in public plazas. Their parents no longer told them what to do. Pretty girls pointed at them. Older men avoided them. They had respect.<br>âYouâll get yourself killed,â I warned Palillo when he mentioned joining.<br>He shrugged. âBetter to die from a bullet than boredom.â<br><br><i>The beginning of the end</i><br>Caraquemada raised the pistol and touched his finger to the trigger.<br>PapÃ¡ cried out, âLook after your mother.â<br>âNo. No. No!â I screamed, bucking desperately from the ground. But Caraquemada fired. I heard the hollow pop of the pistol and watched my fatherâs head jerk suddenly. Then he teetered on his feet for an impossibly long time before tilting forward slowly like a tree dealt its final axe-blow.<br>âLetâs move out!â ordered Caraquemada, turning away.<br>How strange, I thought, that PapÃ¡ did not try to break his fall. His knees buckled and I heard MamÃ¡ wailing as she dashed from the house, and then a terrible crack as PapÃ¡âs skull struck the hard, dry earth.<br><br><i>His father's death forced Pedro to side with the Paramilitaries. He wanted to hunt down all the Guerrilla soldiers responsible for killing his father.<br>He undergoes basic training, learns how to kill with knife and gun, and toughens his body to be able to carry out his personal revenge.<br>Peace talks stop the Army from fighting the Guerrilla, but the Paramilitary continues the war.</i> <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576916247i/28645491._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>This would be an exciting story even if it was fiction - but it was <i>real</i> and that made it most chilling</b><br><br>The good people of the Havelock &amp; Baker publishing house gave me a copy of "Columbiano" in return for my unbiased review. I must admit that this is not my favorite genre, but after reading the first chapter I was caught up in following the fates of these child soldiers.<br><br>Enjoy!<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="5410511580624941209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2948531798" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2948531798" />
    <a title="Lorna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6700518-lorna"><img alt="Lorna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552336346p2/6700518.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2948531798?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorna" href="/user/show/6700518-lorna">Lorna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/43861962-colombiano">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6700518-lorna?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6700518-lorna?shelf=goodreads-authors-giveaways">goodreads-authors-giveaways</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2948531798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16362055430307070565"><i>Colombiano</i> is a riveting look at Colombia, South America following the reign of terror of drug lord Pablo Escobar and the Medellin with the illegal trafficking of cocaine and all of the associated crime and human carnage and devastation. Rusty Young is from Australia and was in Colombia for four years secretly working for the government of the United States in Colombia. It was during this time that he became aware of the shocking stories of the child soldiers and he vowed that one day he would t</span>
  <span id="freeText16362055430307070565" style="display:none"><i>Colombiano</i> is a riveting look at Colombia, South America following the reign of terror of drug lord Pablo Escobar and the Medellin with the illegal trafficking of cocaine and all of the associated crime and human carnage and devastation. Rusty Young is from Australia and was in Colombia for four years secretly working for the government of the United States in Colombia. It was during this time that he became aware of the shocking stories of the child soldiers and he vowed that one day he would tell their compelling stories and let their voices be heard.<br /><br />Pedro Gutierrez is a bright and loving fifteen-year old boy, happy with his parents on their little farm, their <i>finca</i> and in love with his girlfriend Camilla. These are the halcyon days when Pedro loves his village, fishing trips with his father, hanging out with his best friend, Palillo, and planning his future on the family farm. Then one night his world is shattered when the Guerillas come to their farm accusing his father of being a traitor and assassinate him in cold blood while Pedro watches in horror. He takes mental notes of the killers and vows that he will avenge his father's death. He and Palillo join the paramilitary, the <i>Autodefensa</i> and his life is forever changed. They vow also to rid their community, Llorona, of the Guerillas and all of the crime associated with their occupation. This is a riveting and in many ways a violent book but I am afraid very accurate in its portrayal. However, it is also a story of love and loyalty and family, as well as the exploration of vengeance and what can result. This was an outstanding book, perhaps a little long but it was a quick read. The characters were well developed and the sense of place compelling. Clearly the author, Rusty Young, was successful in his mission of telling the stories of the child soldier in a searing and unforgettable way. Mr. Young says it best in the following quotes from the <i>Author's Prologue</i> in his book:<br /><br /><i>"The more emotionally involved I became with the child soldiers' stories, the harder I found it to maintain any pretence of journalistic objectivity. Ultimately, I decided to weave their stories into a novel."<br /><br />"These children's pasts were complicated and painful. Their stories affected me deeply and changed my life. I felt they needed to be told."</i><br /><br />I would like to thank Lily and Havelock &amp; Baker Publishers for kindly providing me with an advanced reader's copy in exchange for my thoughts on the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16362055430307070565" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16109401450227845014">Colombiano is a big book, a very big book both in length and scope, but donât let the page count or the topic put you off. Itâs a well written, wholly absorbing account of a boy soldierâs experience of the guerilla/paramilitary warfare that nearly ripped apart and destroyed Colombia.<br/><br/>Pedro GutiÃ©rrez was only fifteen when he watched guerrilla soldiers march into his familyâs farm and kill his father, a good and honest man. Vowing revenge and too young to join the army, Pedro and his friend Palillo</span>
  <span id="freeText16109401450227845014" style="display:none">Colombiano is a big book, a very big book both in length and scope, but donât let the page count or the topic put you off. Itâs a well written, wholly absorbing account of a boy soldierâs experience of the guerilla/paramilitary warfare that nearly ripped apart and destroyed Colombia.<br /><br />Pedro GutiÃ©rrez was only fifteen when he watched guerrilla soldiers march into his familyâs farm and kill his father, a good and honest man. Vowing revenge and too young to join the army, Pedro and his friend Palillo joined the paramilitary Autodefencas. Surviving brutal basic training alongside other teenagers and children, Pedro eventually wins the trust of those in command and sets about hunting down the men who killed his father and continue to terrorize his village. <br /><br />With insight gained from years of working undercover in Colombia and more recently of interviewing ex-child soldiers, Rusty Young has written a powerful account of the Colombian conflict driven originally by politics and fuelled by money and cocaine. Itâs a hard book to read as the ongoing brutality of what people will do to each other is very intense and I found I had to put the book down quite often for a breather. However, the violence is based on real accounts and is essential in the telling of the horrors these young soldiers had to endure in this very real conflict. Although the characters become hardened to the violence, they never lose their basic humanity in caring for each other and their families, as Pedro shows once his thirst for revenge has been assuaged. <br /><br />This is not a book I would have thought to choose for myself, so I am very grateful that Havelock &amp; Baker Publishing offered me a copy to read. It's a book I will keep thinking about for some time.<br /><br />For those wanting to know more about the author and why he wrote this book, here is a link to an interview from an Australian news program <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_VPWL-YPYsY">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_VPWL...</a></span>

<div id="review_2930362453" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2930362453" />
    <a title="Carolyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5349403-carolyn"><img alt="Carolyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552774381p2/5349403.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2930362453?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carolyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carolyn" href="/user/show/5349403-carolyn">Carolyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2930362453" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11903155059657073927">Many thanks to NetGalley and Havelock & Baker for providing me with this engrossing, intense ARC in return for an honest review. This was a powerful epic which featured violence, family devotion, moral choices, young love, and a country ripped apart. <br/><br/> I found the story informative and was totally absorbed. I had been in Colombia a very short time in a small town on the Amazon, but my knowledge of the country was severely limited. I knew a bit about Pablo Escobar from Netflix, and how the country</span>
  <span id="freeText11903155059657073927" style="display:none">Many thanks to NetGalley and Havelock &amp; Baker for providing me with this engrossing, intense ARC in return for an honest review. This was a powerful epic which featured violence, family devotion, moral choices, young love, and a country ripped apart. <br /><br /> I found the story informative and was totally absorbed. I had been in Colombia a very short time in a small town on the Amazon, but my knowledge of the country was severely limited. I knew a bit about Pablo Escobar from Netflix, and how the country was the centre of jungle drug labs, cocaine manufacturing and distribution. I was aware of political turmoil, guerrilla insurgents, death squads, kidnapping, which made it a dangerous place. Reading the book gave me a greater understanding of the country, and how itâs people often had to go against their moral values to survive. <br /><br /> The almost 700 pages was not a deterrent, as I was gripped by the exciting, suspenseful plot from the beginning. Everything was important to understand the extreme dangers, heartbreaking losses, and the mindsets of the characters. <br /><br />Pedro Gutierrez was a normal fifteen-year-old boy who enjoyed helping on the family farm, going fishing with his father, attending church with his mother, and hanging out with his girlfriend, Camila, and with his best friend, Palillo. One terrible day he witnessed his father brutally murdered by guerrilla soldiers. He and his mother were ordered off their farm and forbidden to give the father a religious burial on consecrated grounds. Pedro vowed revenge on his fatherâs killers. <br /><br /> This led Pedro with his friend, Palillo, to voluntarily join the Autodefencas, a paramilitary group where they endured brutal training and rose in the ranks. Pedro witnessed barbaric torture of guerrilla fighters and their deadly retaliation. The training led to a moral descent and hardened him for vengeance for his fatherâs death. Thoughts of revenge became an obsession. <br /><br />Paramilitary groups, which included the Autodefencas, were comprised mainly of wealthy land or business owners who formed private armies and death squads. Both the paramilitaries and the guerrilla fighters trained and utilized child soldiers. Some were tricked or kidnapped into the militia. Others joined willingly, preferring the training camps to abusive situations at home. <br /><br />The Guerrillas consisted mainly of peasants. They claimed to be fighting against poverty and social inequality with the aim of toppling the government and replacing it with communism. <br /><br /> The author, Rusty Young, interviewed child soldiers in Colombia. This is a brilliant work of fiction and has a definite ring of truth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11903155059657073927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3165999192?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whispering Stories" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whispering Stories" href="/user/show/20354973-whispering-stories">Whispering Stories</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3165999192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10610372272788936649">Book Reviewed on <a href='https://www.whisperingstories.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>www.whisperingstories.com</a><br/><br/>Author Rusty Young having worked for the US government in Columbia was shocked by the stories of child soldiers and vowed he write a book about their tales and Colombiano is just that.<br/><br/>This is a very harrowing book about a young lad, Pedro GutiÃ©rrez, whose world was changed by the killing of his Papa and him seeking revenge. Not all of Pedroâs decisions were right but reading how things were at that time in the rural villages; people being held ransom, dr</span>
  <span id="freeText10610372272788936649" style="display:none">Book Reviewed on <a href="https://www.whisperingstories.com" rel="nofollow noopener">www.whisperingstories.com</a><br /><br />Author Rusty Young having worked for the US government in Columbia was shocked by the stories of child soldiers and vowed he write a book about their tales and Colombiano is just that.<br /><br />This is a very harrowing book about a young lad, Pedro GutiÃ©rrez, whose world was changed by the killing of his Papa and him seeking revenge. Not all of Pedroâs decisions were right but reading how things were at that time in the rural villages; people being held ransom, drugs, drug traffickers, murders and a lot worse! I can see why young people, just like Pedro, did what they did to survive.<br /><br />Colombiano is a very emotional, heart-rendering, epic tale. At times it is almost too hard to read given the shocking violence especially Pedro going after his fatherâs killers and the undeniable treatment the Autodefensas and what the guerrillas did to people.<br /><br />Then we have the love Pedro has for his best friend Palillo, his Mama, and his girlfriend Camila. All these characters are so effortlessly described by Rusty Young. I felt that I knew them and could understand why they behaved as they did. It made me realise how lucky I am to live the life I have!<br /><br />This is a magnificent, powerful read that even with the very worst of shocking, somewhat unbelievable, violence I couldnât stop reading. The knowledge the author has of life in the Colombian villages and his imagination has led to this remarkable book.<br /><br />This is certainly not my normal read and it took me right out of my comfort zone but well done Rusty Young on such a superb book. I will definitely recommend it and I award 5 stars.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14041775786413843585">"Its strange to think that a quick decision taken in the heat of a single moment could change the course of a boys life".<br/><br/>And that one quote sums up 700 pages of text.<br/><br/>This is the story for Pedro and his friends, all teenagers, all for various reasons are tangled up in war funded by cocaine, criminals and the corrupt. This is the story of how ruthless human beings can be. <br/><br/>I haven't read much on Colombia and from the first chapter I was pulled in, this book is so readable the words flow as easily a</span>
  <span id="freeText14041775786413843585" style="display:none">"Its strange to think that a quick decision taken in the heat of a single moment could change the course of a boys life".<br /><br />And that one quote sums up 700 pages of text.<br /><br />This is the story for Pedro and his friends, all teenagers, all for various reasons are tangled up in war funded by cocaine, criminals and the corrupt. This is the story of how ruthless human beings can be. <br /><br />I haven't read much on Colombia and from the first chapter I was pulled in, this book is so readable the words flow as easily as the Colombian rivers mentioned. As someone who doesn't mind reading a bit of gore I had to put this book down on many occasions due to the graphic content, maybe because it's based on true stories, maybe because its teenagers or maybe because it's so damn real I felt I was there. <br /><br />This book made me think of all the wars going on today, all the children being trained as fighters, the corruption, the blind eyes. <br /><br />I felt a lot of things reading this book but not the 700 pages. This book is a journey that deserves every star available.</span>

  <span id="freeText14820140778203605652" style="display:none"><b>4.5â<br />âWhen I was eight, the boy Iâd sat next to in class was hit by a truck. Devastated, I refused to go to school. But knowing there had been a time before our friendship made it possible to imagine a time after. A parent is different. There is no time before a parent. A parent is always.â</b><br /><br />This is a long, terrifying look at the worst of the disaster that awaits those in Colombia who dare to cross the . . . well, the army, the Guerrillas, or the paramilitary vigilantes that form a third force. All of them seem to get mixed up in or affected by the drug trade somewhere along the line, and the children who are gathered into the fold by Guerrillas and paramilitary forces are indoctrinated young.<br /><br />The Australian-born author lived in Colombia and spent a lot of time with the youngsters about whom this is written. It is written as fiction, but told in the first person by Pedro, who's just lost his father and canât imagine life without him. I would say this is something like a ghost-written autobiography, but with the addition of conversations, romance, and the fierce feelings of a teen-aged boy who grows old and jaded before his time.<br /><br />He gives us good background on how Colombians ended up stuck not just between a rock and hard place, but between a rock, a hard place, and an impossible place. There is no way out. Elections and government are far removed from the villages where the action takes place. Sure, they have them, but itâs like the Mafia â pay your ever-increasing protection money, and we wonât burn your place down and torture you.<br /><br />They call these <i>âvacunasâ</i>, vaccinations â which should be protection enough, right? But what happens when youâve paid the guerrilla forces but not someone else whoâs threatening you? <br /><br /><b>âBut in a country with two terrorist organisations whose members numbered in the tens of thousands, it didnât pay to advertise my job. The first group was the FARC Guerrilla. In the 1960s, peasant farmers took up arms, aiming to fight poverty and social inequality by toppling the government and installing communist rule. To fund their revolution, they âtaxedâ businesses and kidnapped the rich, appropriating their lands for redistribution to the poor. <br /><br />The second group â the Paramilitaries â was created in response. Wealthy land and business owners, tired of the governmentâs failure to protect them, formed their own private militias and âdeath squadsâ.â</b><br /><br />Pedro is a kid, fifteen, and the only way he can fight against the Guerrillas who killed his father is to join the paramilitaries, the <i>Autodefensas</i>, since heâs too young for the army. He and his friends live in the small town of Llorona.<br /><br /><b>âIn Llorona, we didnât call them <i>Autodefensas</i> or Paramilitaries, or even <i>paras</i> or <i>paracos</i> like they did in newspapers. They were simply <i>los duros</i> â the hard men. The <i>duros</i> were the archenemy of the Guerrilla. Fearsome hit squads committed to wiping out communism, theyâd been founded in cities and worked their way into towns and villages, then outwards into the mountains where Guerrilla bases were located.ââ</b><br /><br />And why do they have this overwhelming need to join up and fight? Hereâs the Guerrilla leader talking to Pedroâs mother<br /><br /><b>âZorrillo looked from MamÃ¡ to me. <i>âWe know your names. We know where you â¦â</i> he paused to smirk, <i>âused to live. We have people everywhere watching. Thereâs nowhere in this country we wonât find you. The penalty for defiance is death.â</i> He fired his rifle into the air. <i>âÂ¡Viva la revoluciÃ³n!â</i>â</b><br /><br />I will not quote any of the horrific, blood-curdling descriptions of what happens to those who break the rules. Alongside those are poignant episodes about loyalty, tender teen-age love, friends protecting each other and their families, and the almost adoptive approach that some superiors took to the young kids in their charge â until they broke some rule. Incidentally, they recruit both boys and girls.<br /><br />Itâs scary stuff, well-written with great feeling, and an exciting story. It is long, and I found myself skimming some sections in the middle, wondering who those editors are who condense books for The Readerâs Digest. Somehow, an exceptional editor can tighten up a long story and make it more accessible to readers without losing the essence. <br /><br />The politics, the intrigue, the double-dealing, the change of loyalties â itâs all here, and itâs all terrifying. Again, I feel lucky to have had the life Iâve had. From the authorâs epilogue:<br /><br /><b>âAt the peak of the war, an estimated 11,000 to 14,000 children were involved in the conflict, a third of whom were girls. The former soldiers, some as young as eight when they joined, described in detail their reasons for enlisting, their hatred of the enemy, their gruelling military training, their political indoctrination and their horrific experiences in battle.â</b><br /><br />Itâs an excellent book, published in 2017, and I congratulate the author on his work. I also thank NetGalley and Havelock and Baker Publishing for my copy for review.<br /><br />Thereâs an interview with the author here:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.abc.net.au/radio/programs/conversations/conversations-rusty-young/8739626">https://www.abc.net.au/radio/programs...</a><br /></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('53XfwCsvtbkvR0e/xPShPbDkHKtJKBQ3OBftRNH0DXd1qQOfQ+iGR5uXmFIzj1nCLsIbiGcuombRz3aa3RfQeg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('eKD4e/DuJnOhppLt5GaegWEHk4Ig2ZVGga0aOjUKD6LqfCQkmCkVjRV2TQATHWZ+/yGUoQ7fIxdodYHkOenSrw==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9497854128722775066"><b>Rusty Young</b> (born 1975) is the Australian-born author of the international bestseller <i>Marching Powder</i>, the true story of an English drug smuggler in Boliviaâs notorious San Pedro Prison and the bestselling novel, <i>Colombiano</i>, a fact-meets-fiction revenge thriller about a Colombian boy who sets out to avenge his fatherâs death.<br/> <br/>Rusty grew up in Sydney, and studied Finance and Law at the University o</span>
  <span id="freeText9497854128722775066" style="display:none"><b>Rusty Young</b> (born 1975) is the Australian-born author of the international bestseller <i>Marching Powder</i>, the true story of an English drug smuggler in Boliviaâs notorious San Pedro Prison and the bestselling novel, <i>Colombiano</i>, a fact-meets-fiction revenge thriller about a Colombian boy who sets out to avenge his fatherâs death.<br /> <br />Rusty grew up in Sydney, and studied Finance and Law at the University of New South Wales. He was backpacking in South America when he heard about Thomas McFadden, a convicted English drug trafficker who ran tours inside Bolivia's famous San Pedro Prison. Curious about the reason behind McFadden's huge popularity, the law graduate went to La Paz and joined one of Thomas's illegal tours. They formed an instant friendship and then became partners in an attempt to record Thomas's experiences in the jail.<br /> <br />Rusty bribed the guards to allow him to stay and for the next three months he lived inside the prison, sharing a cell with Thomas. After securing Thomas's release, Rusty lived in Colombia where he taught the English language and wrote Thomas's story. The memoir, <i>Marching Powder</i>, was released in 2003 and was an international bestseller.<br /> <br />Following the success of <i>Marching Powder</i>, Rusty was recruited as a Program Director of the US government's Anti-Kidnapping Program in Colombia. He was part of a team that trained local police, military and SWAT teams in kidnapping response and hostage rescue. At the time, Colombia had an average of eight kidnappings a day. It was a role fraught with danger and Rusty lived part-time on a military base, drove a Level III armoured vehicle, communicated with colleagues via encrypted radio and changed houses in BogotÃ¡ a dozen times. He kept this work completely secret.<br /> <br />Through police and army contacts, Rusty was able to interview special forces soldiers, including snipers and undercover intelligence agents, about their work. He also interviewed captured child soldiers from the two main terrorist organisations â FARC and Autodefensas. The former soldiers, some as young at twelve when they joined, described in great detail their reasons for enlisting, their hatred of the enemy, their gruelling military training, their political indoctrination and their horrific experiences in battle. Once Rusty had earned their trust, they also opened up to him about gruesome tortures they were forced to witness or participate in.<br /> <br />These interviews, along with Rustyâs extensive in-the-field knowledge about cocaine trafficking, formed the factual setting and background for his novel <i>Colombiano</i>, a fact-meets-fiction revenge thriller.<br /> <br /><i>Colombiano</i> was published in Australia in August 2017 and was the highest-selling Australian fiction title that month. It will be released worldwide in 2018.<br /> <br />In 2011, Rusty co-founded the Colombian Childrenâs Foundation of Australia, which helps rehabilitate and resocialise former child soldiers. Currently, his house in BogotÃ¡ is the charityâs headquarters. Ten percent of his royalties of <i>Colombiano</i> will go to the foundation, which has almost 200 former child soldiers under care.<br /> <br />Rusty also fronts the documentary <i>Wildlands</i> (2017) in which he interviews notorious characters formerly involved in the cocaine trade, including George Jung â famously played by Johnny Depp in the movie Blow â and, more terrifyingly, John Velasquez or âPopeyeâ, Pablo Escobarâs right-hand man and one of the deadliest hitmen in cartel history.</span>
